class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><p>Symantec’s Threat Hunter Team has discovered that at least one affiliate of the BlackByte ransomware (Ransom.Blackbyte) operation has begun using a custom data exfiltration tool during their attacks. The malware (Infostealer.Exbyte) is designed to expedite the theft of data from the victim’s network and upload it to an external server.</p>

<p>BlackByte is a ransomware-as-a-service operation that is run by a cyber-crime group Symantec calls Hecamede. The group sprang to public attention in February 2022 when <a href="https://www.ic3.gov/Media/News/2022/220211.pdf">the U.S. Federal Bureau of Investigation (FBI) issued an alert</a> stating that BlackByte had been used to attack multiple entities in the U.S., including organizations in at least three critical infrastructure sectors. In recent months, BlackByte has become one of the most frequently used payloads in ransomware attacks.</p>

<h2>Inside Exbyte</h2>

<p>The Exbyte exfiltration tool is written in Go and designed to upload stolen files to the Mega.co.nz cloud storage service.</p>

<p>On execution, Exbyte performs a series of checks for indicators that it may be running in a sandboxed environment. This is intended to make it more difficult for security researchers to analyze the malware. To do this, it calls the IsDebuggerPresent and CheckRemoteDebuggerPresent APIs. It then checks for the running processes from the following applications:</p>

<ul>
	<li>MegaDumper 1.0 by CodeCracker / SnD</li>
	<li>Import reconstructor</li>
	<li>x64dbg</li>
	<li>x32dbg</li>
	<li>OLLYDBG</li>
	<li>WinDbg</li>
	<li>The Interactive Disassembler</li>
	<li>Immunity Debugger -&nbsp;[CPU]</li>
</ul>

<p>It then checks for the following anti-virus or sandbox-related files:</p>

<ul>
	<li>avghooka.dll</li>
	<li>avghookx.dll</li>
	<li>sxin.dll</li>
	<li>sf2.dll</li>
	<li>sbiedll.dll</li>
	<li>snxhk.dll</li>
	<li>cmdvrt32.dll</li>
	<li>cmdvrt64.dll</li>
	<li>wpespy.dll</li>
	<li>vmcheck.dll</li>
	<li>pstorec.dll</li>
	<li>dir_watch.dll</li>
	<li>api_log.dll</li>
	<li>dbghelp.dll</li>
</ul>

<p>This routine of checks is quite similar to the routine employed by the BlackByte payload itself, <a href="https://news.sophos.com/en-us/2022/10/04/blackbyte-ransomware-returns/">as documented recently by Sophos</a>.</p>

<p>Next, Exbyte enumerates all document files on the infected computer, such as .txt, .doc, and .pdf files, and saves the full path and file name to %APPDATA%\dummy. The files listed are then uploaded to a folder the malware creates on Mega.co.nz. Credentials for the Mega account used are hardcoded into Exbyte.</p>

<p>Exbyte is not the first custom-developed data exfiltration tool to be linked to a ransomware operation. In November 2021, <a href="https://symantec-enterprise-blogs.security.com/blogs/threat-intelligence/blackmatter-data-exfiltration">Symantec discovered Exmatter</a>, an exfiltration tool that was used by the BlackMatter ransomware operation and <a href="https://symantec-enterprise-blogs.security.com/blogs/threat-intelligence/noberus-blackcat-ransomware-ttps">has since been used in Noberus attacks</a>. Other examples include the Ryuk Stealer tool and StealBit, which is linked to the LockBit ransomware.</p>

<h2>BlackByte TTPs</h2>

<p>In recent BlackByte attacks investigated by Symantec, the attackers exploited the ProxyShell (<a href="https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/vulnerability/CVE-2021-34473">CVE-2021-34473</a>,&nbsp;<a href="https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/vulnerability/CVE-2021-34523">CVE-2021-34523</a>, and&nbsp;<a href="https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/en-US/vulnerability/CVE-2021-31207">CVE-2021-31207</a>) and ProxyLogon (<a href="https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/en-US/vulnerability/CVE-2021-26855">CVE-2021-26855</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a href="https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/en-US/vulnerability/CVE-2021-27065">CVE-2021-27065</a>) vulnerabilities in Microsoft Exchange Servers to gain initial access.</p>

<p>Symantec has observed attackers using AdFind, AnyDesk, NetScan, and PowerView prior to deploying the ransomware payload.</p>

<p>Recent attacks have used version 2.0 of the BlackByte payload. On execution, the ransomware payload itself appears to download and save debugging symbols from Microsoft. The command is executed directly from the ransomware:</p>

<p><em>powershell -command "(New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadFile('http://msdl.microsoft.com/download/symbols/ntkrnlmp.pdb/11D60DB07BA7433B923F49867DF515721/ntkrnlmp.pdb', 'CSIDL_SYSTEM_DRIVE\systemdata\ntkrnlmp.pdb')"</em></p>

<p>The ransomware then checks the version information of ntoskrnl.exe and then creates a service with the following details:</p>

<p><em>binPath = C:\systemdata\generalate</em></p>

<p><em>displayName = AAAAAAAAAAAAAA!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!</em></p>

<p>BlackByte then proceeds with the removal of Kernel Notify Routines. The purpose of this is to attempt to bypass EDR products. This functionality in BlackByte <a href="https://news.sophos.com/en-us/2022/10/04/blackbyte-ransomware-returns/">has already been documented by Sophos</a> and it closely resembles the techniques leveraged in <a href="https://github.com/wavestone-cdt/EDRSandblast">the EDRSandblast tool</a>.</p>

<p>BlackByte uses VssAdmin to delete volume shadow copies and resize storage allocation:</p>

<p><em>cmd.exe /c start vssadmin.exe Delete Shadows /All /Quiet</em></p>

<p><em>vssadmin Resize ShadowStorage /For=K: /On=K: /MaxSize=401MB</em></p>

<p>It then makes the following service modifications:</p>

<p><em>sc create ODosTEmONa binPath= CSIDL_SYSTEM_DRIVE\systemdata\generalate type= kernel</em></p>

<p><em>sc.exe config RemoteRegistry start= auto</em></p>

<p><em>sc.exe config Dnscache start= auto</em></p>

<p><em>sc.exe config SSDPSRV start= auto</em></p>

<p><em>sc.exe config fdPHost start= auto</em></p>

<p><em>sc.exe config upnphost start= auto</em></p>

<p>The ransomware then modifies firewall settings to enable linked connections:</p>

<p><em>netsh advfirewall firewall set rule "group=\"Network Discovery\" " new enable=Yes"</em></p>

<p><em>netsh advfirewall firewall set rule "group=\"File and Printer Sharing\" " new enable=Yes"</em></p>

<p><em>cmd.exe /c netsh advfirewall set allprofiles state off</em></p>

<p>Finally, BlackByte injects itself into an instance of svchost.exe, conducts file encryption, and then deletes the ransomware binary on disk:</p>

<p><em>cmd.exe /c ping 1.1.1.1 -n 10 &gt; Nul &amp; Del CSIDL_WINDOWS\rdac.exe /F /Q</em></p>

<p><em>CSIDL_SYSTEM\svchost.exe -s 27262842</em></p>

<h2>Emerging Force</h2>

<p>Following the departure of a number of major ransomware operations such as Conti and Sodinokibi, BlackByte has emerged as one of the ransomware actors to profit from this gap in the market. The fact that actors are now creating custom tools for use in BlackByte attacks suggests that it may be on the way to becoming one of the dominant ransomware threats.</p>

<h2>Protection/Mitigation</h2>

<p>For the latest protection updates, please visit the <a href="https://www.broadcom.com/support/security-center/protection-bulletin">Symantec Protection Bulletin</a>.</p>

<p>&nbsp;</p>

<p><strong>Yara Rule</strong></p>

<p>rule blackbyte_exfil</p>

<p>{</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; meta:</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; copyright = "Symantec"</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; family = "Alias:ExfilTool"</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; description = "Detects exfil tool used by BlackByte ransomware"</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; strings:</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; $data_str1 = {41 B9 04 00 00 00 66 66 0F 1F 84 00 00 00 00 00</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 43 0F B6 84 02 A0 00 00 00 41 30 00 49 FF C0 49</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 83 E9 01 75 EB 49 83 EB 01 75 D5 40 B7 09 48 8D}</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; $data_str2 = {32 10 05 AF 59 2E 0D 38 32 59 C0 99 E8 A5 87 CB}</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; $data_str3 = "@BCEFHJLNPRTVY" ascii</p>

<p>&nbsp;</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; condition:</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; all of ($data_str*)</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; and filesize &gt; 2MB and filesize &lt; 3MB and</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; (uint16(0) == 0x5A4D and uint16(uint32(0x3c)) == 0x4550)</p>

<p>}</p>

<p>&nbsp;</p>

<p>rule blackbyte_exfil_unpacked</p>

<p>{</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; meta:</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; copyright = "Symantec"</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; family = "Alias:ExfilTool"</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; description = "Detects unpacked exfil tool used by BlackByte ransomware"</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; strings:</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; $str1 = ").Login"</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; $str2 = ").NewUpload"</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; $str3 = ").CreateDir"</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;$str4 = ".PreloginMsg"</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; $str5 = ".UploadCompleteMsg"</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; $str6 = ").UploadFile"</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp; $str7 = {FF 20 47 6F 20 62 75 69 6C 64 69 6E 66 3A 08 02</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; 07 75 6E 6B 6E 6F 77 6E 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00}</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp; $c1 = {44 24 68 44 31 C2 88 50 10 0F B6 54 24 56 44 0F}</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp; $c2 = {FB 48 89 F7 4C 89 C6 E8 54 ED F6 FF 4C 8D 43 01}</p>

<p>&nbsp;</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; condition:</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; all of ($str*) and ($c1 or $c2)</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; and filesize &gt; 8MB&nbsp; and</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; (uint16(0) == 0x5A4D and uint16(uint32(0x3c)) == 0x4550)</p>

<p>}</p>

The malware (Infostealer.Exbyte) is designed to expedite the theft of data from the victim’s network and upload it to an external server.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte is a ransomware-as-a-service operation that is run by a cyber-crime group Symantec calls Hecamede. The group sprang to public attention in February 2022 when \u003ca href=\"https://www.ic3.gov/Media/News/2022/220211.pdf\"\u003ethe U.S. Federal Bureau of Investigation (FBI) issued an alert\u003c/a\u003e stating that BlackByte had been used to attack multiple entities in the U.S., including organizations in at least three critical infrastructure sectors. In recent months, BlackByte has become one of the most frequently used payloads in ransomware attacks.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eInside Exbyte\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe Exbyte exfiltration tool is written in Go and designed to upload stolen files to the Mega.co.nz cloud storage service.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eOn execution, Exbyte performs a series of checks for indicators that it may be running in a sandboxed environment. This is intended to make it more difficult for security researchers to analyze the malware. To do this, it calls the IsDebuggerPresent and CheckRemoteDebuggerPresent APIs. It then checks for the running processes from the following applications:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eMegaDumper 1.0 by CodeCracker / SnD\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eImport reconstructor\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003ex64dbg\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003ex32dbg\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eOLLYDBG\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eWinDbg\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe Interactive Disassembler\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eImmunity Debugger -\u0026nbsp;[CPU]\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIt then checks for the following anti-virus or sandbox-related files:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eavghooka.dll\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eavghookx.dll\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003esxin.dll\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003esf2.dll\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003esbiedll.dll\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003esnxhk.dll\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003ecmdvrt32.dll\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003ecmdvrt64.dll\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003ewpespy.dll\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003evmcheck.dll\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003epstorec.dll\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003edir_watch.dll\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eapi_log.dll\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003edbghelp.dll\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThis routine of checks is quite similar to the routine employed by the BlackByte payload itself, \u003ca href=\"https://news.sophos.com/en-us/2022/10/04/blackbyte-ransomware-returns/\"\u003eas documented recently by Sophos\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eNext, Exbyte enumerates all document files on the infected computer, such as .txt, .doc, and .pdf files, and saves the full path and file name to %APPDATA%\\dummy. The files listed are then uploaded to a folder the malware creates on Mega.co.nz. Credentials for the Mega account used are hardcoded into Exbyte.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eExbyte is not the first custom-developed data exfiltration tool to be linked to a ransomware operation. In November 2021, \u003ca href=\"https://symantec-enterprise-blogs.security.com/blogs/threat-intelligence/blackmatter-data-exfiltration\"\u003eSymantec discovered Exmatter\u003c/a\u003e, an exfiltration tool that was used by the BlackMatter ransomware operation and \u003ca href=\"https://symantec-enterprise-blogs.security.com/blogs/threat-intelligence/noberus-blackcat-ransomware-ttps\"\u003ehas since been used in Noberus attacks\u003c/a\u003e. Other examples include the Ryuk Stealer tool and StealBit, which is linked to the LockBit ransomware.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eBlackByte TTPs\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn recent BlackByte attacks investigated by Symantec, the attackers exploited the ProxyShell (\u003ca href=\"https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/vulnerability/CVE-2021-34473\"\u003eCVE-2021-34473\u003c/a\u003e,\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/vulnerability/CVE-2021-34523\"\u003eCVE-2021-34523\u003c/a\u003e, and\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/en-US/vulnerability/CVE-2021-31207\"\u003eCVE-2021-31207\u003c/a\u003e) and ProxyLogon (\u003ca href=\"https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/en-US/vulnerability/CVE-2021-26855\"\u003eCVE-2021-26855\u003c/a\u003e\u0026nbsp;and\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/en-US/vulnerability/CVE-2021-27065\"\u003eCVE-2021-27065\u003c/a\u003e) vulnerabilities in Microsoft Exchange Servers to gain initial access.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eSymantec has observed attackers using AdFind, AnyDesk, NetScan, and PowerView prior to deploying the ransomware payload.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eRecent attacks have used version 2.0 of the BlackByte payload. On execution, the ransomware payload itself appears to download and save debugging symbols from Microsoft. The command is executed directly from the ransomware:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003epowershell -command \"(New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadFile('http://msdl.microsoft.com/download/symbols/ntkrnlmp.pdb/11D60DB07BA7433B923F49867DF515721/ntkrnlmp.pdb', 'CSIDL_SYSTEM_DRIVE\\systemdata\\ntkrnlmp.pdb')\"\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe ransomware then checks the version information of ntoskrnl.exe and then creates a service with the following details:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003ebinPath = C:\\systemdata\\generalate\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003edisplayName = AAAAAAAAAAAAAA!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte then proceeds with the removal of Kernel Notify Routines. The purpose of this is to attempt to bypass EDR products. This functionality in BlackByte \u003ca href=\"https://news.sophos.com/en-us/2022/10/04/blackbyte-ransomware-returns/\"\u003ehas already been documented by Sophos\u003c/a\u003e and it closely resembles the techniques leveraged in \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/wavestone-cdt/EDRSandblast\"\u003ethe EDRSandblast tool\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBlackByte uses VssAdmin to delete volume shadow copies and resize storage allocation:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003ecmd.exe /c start vssadmin.exe Delete Shadows /All /Quiet\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003evssadmin Resize ShadowStorage /For=K: /On=K: /MaxSize=401MB\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIt then makes the following service modifications:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003esc create ODosTEmONa binPath= CSIDL_SYSTEM_DRIVE\\systemdata\\generalate type= kernel\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003esc.exe config RemoteRegistry start= auto\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003esc.exe config Dnscache start= auto\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003esc.exe config SSDPSRV start= auto\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003esc.exe config fdPHost start= auto\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003esc.exe config upnphost start= auto\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe ransomware then modifies firewall settings to enable linked connections:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003enetsh advfirewall firewall set rule \"group=\\\"Network Discovery\\\" \" new enable=Yes\"\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003enetsh advfirewall firewall set rule \"group=\\\"File and Printer Sharing\\\" \" new enable=Yes\"\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003ecmd.exe /c netsh advfirewall set allprofiles state off\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFinally, BlackByte injects itself into an instance of svchost.exe, conducts file encryption, and then deletes the ransomware binary on disk:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003ecmd.exe /c ping 1.1.1.1 -n 10 \u0026gt; Nul \u0026amp; Del CSIDL_WINDOWS\\rdac.exe /F /Q\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eCSIDL_SYSTEM\\svchost.exe -s 27262842\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eEmerging Force\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFollowing the departure of a number of major ransomware operations such as Conti and Sodinokibi, BlackByte has emerged as one of the ransomware actors to profit from this gap in the market. The fact that actors are now creating custom tools for use in BlackByte attacks suggests that it may be on the way to becoming one of the dominant ransomware threats.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eProtection/Mitigation\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFor the latest protection updates, please visit the \u003ca APT group Jewelbug (aka REF7707, CL-STA-0049, Earth Alux) has been highly active in recent months, targeting organizations in South America, South Asia, Taiwan and Russia. One of its intrusions was on the network of a Russian IT service provider and lasted for the first five months of 2025.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAttackers had access to code repository and software build systems that they could potentially leverage to carry out supply chain attacks targeting the company’s customers in Russia. Notably too, the attackers were exfiltrating data to Yandex Cloud. Yandex is a popular service in Russia, so the attackers likely chose to use it in order to avoid raising suspicions.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn other activity on a large South American government organization in July 2025, Jewelbug deployed a new backdoor that appears to be under development by the group.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug also compromised the network of a Taiwanese company, as well as another IT provider based in South Asia in recent months. However, its targeting of a Russian company is of particular note as Chinese and Russian threat actors have, until recently, rarely been seen to be attacking each other. Jewelbug’s attack is the continuation of a trend that seems to have begun following Russia’s invasion of Ukraine.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAttack on Russian IT service provider\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe first suspicious activity that occurred on this network was the appearance of a file named 7zup.exe (Command line: \u003cem\u003eCSIDL_PROFILE\\public\\downloads\\7zup.exe -d -remote up\u003c/em\u003e), which is a renamed copy of cdb.exe, a benign Microsoft signed binary. CDB is the Microsoft Console Debugger. Use of a renamed version of cbd.exe is a hallmark of Jewelbug activity. CDB can be used to run shellcode and bypass application whitelisting. It can also be used to launch executables, run DLLs and terminate security solutions, making it a powerful tool. Microsoft recommends that CDB should be blocked from running by default and whitelisted for specific users only when it’s explicitly needed.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOther activity on this network included credential dumping, and persistence and elevation of privileges via scheduled tasks (schtasks). The attackers also attempted to cover up their activity by clearing Windows Event Logs.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe use of Yandex Cloud to exfiltrate data was also a probable attempt by the attackers to remain under the radar as Yandex is a legitimate and commonly used cloud service in Russia. For this reason, it is unlikely to be blocked by Russian enterprises, and its use is less likely to raise suspicions. To exfiltrate the victims’ data the attackers used a malicious sample that they had named “yandex2.exe.”\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAs mentioned previously, the attackers were also targeting machines with build systems and code repository systems, potentially seeking to leverage access to the source code to carry out a supply chain attack targeting the company’s customers in Russia. IT service providers are popular targets for attackers seeking to carry out supply chain attacks as they often have extensive access to their customers’ systems and may be able to automatically deploy updates or software across a large number of networks simultaneously, potentially giving the attackers access to, or allowing them to infect, a huge number of organizations at the same time.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt appears attackers may have been on this network for some time, with the first indication of suspicious activity dating from January 2025, while the most recent suspicious activity on this network occurred in May 2025.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eNew backdoor deployed in South American victim\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug also compromised a network belonging to a South American government department. It appears Jewelbug has been on the network of the department multiple times, or else has maintained persistence on the network for a long time, because suspicious activity was first seen in this organization in September 2024, with the most recent activity seen in July 2025.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn the September 2024 activity, the attackers attempted to add a new user to the network, as well as attempting to deploy a remote access tool to gain access to machines. They also used the legitimate AnyDesk remote management software, and deployed the 7-zip archive manager, which is often used to pack files before they are exfiltrated from victim machines. In the more recent activity in July 2025, the attackers used a legitimate executable for DLL sideloading, and the SMBExec tool for likely lateral movement on the network. They also used scheduled tasks for persistence, and BITSAdmin and the curl tool, probably to exfiltrate data.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAlso in this organization, Jewelbug deployed what appears to be a new backdoor in development by the group.\u0026nbsp;This malware leverages Microsoft Graph API and OneDrive as its command and control (C\u0026amp;C) servers. The malware deployed on the victim network appears to have some issues and limitations, perhaps pointing to it being a work in progress, or some inexperience on the part of the developer. However, activity we do see being carried out by this malware includes:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eObtains a list of files from targeted machines and uploads this to OneDrive\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eDoes some logging into C:\\ProgramData\\application.ini. Some examples of this logging includes:\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003einit successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eFreeFileInformation Fuc successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eC:\\Users\\Public\\Libraries~\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCreateDirectory\u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp;Successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eGet Token successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCreate Folder In OneDrive successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eHttpSendRequestWPtr Error Code:0\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCreates the directory: C:\\Users\\Public\\Libraries~ and hides it on the victim machine\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe malware also obtains the infected machine’s IP, Windows version, and hostname. In some cases, it also collects the machine identifier. It uploads this information to OneDrive.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe logging done by this malware is of note as it points to this malware possibly being “tested” by the attackers. It is also notable as it shows that Jewelbug is continuing to develop new malware. The\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.security.com/threat-intelligence/cloud-espionage-attacks\"\u003euse of Microsoft Graph API\u003c/a\u003e and OneDrive for C\u0026amp;C by Jewelbug is also interesting as it minimizes malicious indicators that would be observable to traditional security software, making this activity much harder to detect.\u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eBYOVD technique used to target Taiwanese software company\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug was also on the network of a Taiwanese software company in October and November 2024. Unlike the later activity targeting the Russian IT service provider, however, there was no evidence of a software supply chain attack motivation. The attackers used DLL sideloading to load malware payloads. DLL sideloading is a very popular tactic among Chinese threat groups. ShadowPad was also deployed on this network. ShadowPad is a powerful modular backdoor that is exclusively used by Chinese threat actors. The attackers also used the KillAV tool to disable security software, as well as deploying a publicly available tool called EchoDrv, which permits abuse of the Kernel read/write vulnerability in the ECHOAC anti-cheat driver. This is likely an example of the attackers using the bring-your-own-vulnerable-driver (BYOVD) technique as an attempt to avoid their malicious activity being detected. They also created scheduled tasks for persistence.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe attackers leveraged LSASS and Mimikatz for dumping credentials, and Fast Reverse Proxy, which can expose local servers to the public internet, while they also deployed several publicly available tools for discovery and privilege escalation. The tools (PrintNotifyPotato, Coerced Potato, and Sweet Potato) are all freely available on GitHub. The attackers also used a publicly available tunnelling tool called Earthworm in a likely attempt to mask commands or data being sent to and from the victim network. The attackers were on this network for approximately three weeks, indicating some skill at remaining under the radar.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOn the networks of both the Taiwanese software company and the South American government agency, a renamed version of the Microsoft Console Debugger (cdb.exe) tool was used. Jewelbug’s use of this tool is notable as it is a relatively under the radar tool, despite it having numerous uses that could be beneficial to malicious actors, as discussed above. In one instance we also saw the CDB tool being injected into mspaint.exe. Mspaint has \u003ca href=\"https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/advanced-backdoor-squidoor/\"\u003epreviously been documented\u003c/a\u003e as being used by Jewelbug to inject malware.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug’s preference for using cloud services and other legitimate tools in its operations indicates that remaining under the radar and establishing a stealthy and persistent presence on victim networks is of utmost importance to this group.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003ePrevious Jewelbug activity\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug is believed to have been active since mid-2023, which was when binaries associated with it were first uploaded to VirusTotal. As well as the attacks mentioned in this blog, Jewelbug has also \u003ca href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security-labs/fragile-web-ref7707\"\u003ebeen associated\u003c/a\u003e with attacks targeting organizations in Southeast Asia, including a university, telecoms organization and a government ministry. Up to this point the group has been associated only with attacks on organizations in Asia and South America, there has been no previous documentation of the group targeting organizations in Russia. A \u003ca href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security-labs/fragile-web-ref7707\"\u003eblog released by security company Elastic earlier this year\u003c/a\u003e did say it had seen Jewelbug attackers using a domain that mimicked a Russian organization, but they did not identify any victims in Russia as part of that activity.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat blog also detailed Jewelbug’s use of unique malware called Finaldraft, Pathloader and Guidloader. Finaldraft is a full-featured remote administration tool with the ability to accept add-on modules that extend functionality. It also:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eHas support for proxying network traffic.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eUses third-party Microsoft Graph API for C\u0026amp;C.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eSupports both Windows and Linux versions.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003ePathloader and Guidloader are malware used to download and execute encrypted shellcode in memory. They have only been observed in association with Finaldraft.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/advanced-backdoor-squidoor/\"\u003ePalo Alto also published a report\u003c/a\u003e about the custom malware used by Jewelbug, though it dubbed the Finaldraft backdoor Squidoor. It also noted that Jewelbug gained access to networks of interest by exploiting various vulnerabilities in Internet Information Services (IIS) servers before deploying webshells on infected servers. It also said that as well as using the Microsoft Graph API for C\u0026amp;C communication, the attackers also used DNS and ICMP tunnelling.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003ePrevious reporting about Jewelbug has also noted the use of the CDB tool, the use of mspaint.exe to inject commands, and the wide-ranging use of dual-use and living off the land tools by the attackers as well, all of which was also seen in the recent activity observed by the Threat Hunter Team.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAll indications point to Jewelbug being of Chinese origin, with its motivation most likely to be espionage and maintaining a long-term and stealthy presence on compromised networks.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eChinese threat actors looking in a new direction?\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe most notable element of all this recent Jewelbug activity is the targeting of a Russian IT service provider by the Chinese APT group. When it comes to things like the Russia-Ukraine conflict and other geopolitical matters, China has traditionally backed, or at least not opposed, Russia, with the two considered to be loosely allied. The targeting of a Russian organization by a Chinese APT group shows, however, that Russia is not out-of-bounds when it comes to operations by China-based actors. The fact that there are indications the IT service provider may have been targeted for the purposes of a software supply chain attack on the company’s customers in Russia is also notable as it means this attack had the potential to give the attackers access to a large number of companies in the country, which they could have used for cyber espionage or disruption.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug’s use of a new backdoor-in-development in this set of activity is also important as it shows that the group is continuing to actively develop its toolset and capabilities. Jewelbug, as a relatively new Chinese APT group, is one to watch as it has the skills to develop its own malware and maintain a long-term and stealthy presence on networks.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eIndicators of compromise (IOCs)\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFile 2\u0026gt;\u0026amp;1\u003c/p\u003e"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"24:T2305,"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"\u003cp\u003eThe Warlock ransomware first appeared in June 2025 and made an impact weeks later, after attackers deploying it were discovered \u003ca href=\"https://www.security.com/threat-intelligence/toolshell-zero-day-sharepoint-cve-2025-53770\"\u003eexploiting the ToolShell zero-day vulnerability\u003c/a\u003e in Microsoft SharePoint (\u003ca href=\"https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/vulnerability/CVE-2025-53770\"\u003eCVE-2025-53770\u003c/a\u003e) on July 19, 2025.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWarlock is an unusual threat. Unlike many ransomware operations, which are headquartered in Russia or other countries in the Commonwealth of Independent States, Warlock appears to be used by a group based in China. And, while its name is new, its origins appear to date back much further, with links to a diverse range of activity.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eToolShell attacks\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eWarlock sprang to prominence following the ToolShell attacks. Prior to patching of this vulnerability, Microsoft said that \u003ca href=\"https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2025/07/22/disrupting-active-exploitation-of-on-premises-sharepoint-vulnerabilities/\"\u003ethree distinct actors\u003c/a\u003e, all of which it linked to China, had been exploiting it as a zero-day: Budworm (aka Linen Typhoon, APT27), Sheathminer (aka Violet Typhoon, APT31), and\u0026nbsp;Storm-2603. Storm-2603 was using the exploit to deploy Warlock and another ransomware payload, LockBit. The new Warlock ransomware appears to be unrelated to an \u003ca href=\"https://www.broadcom.com/support/security-center/protection-bulletin/warlock-dark-army-ransomware\"\u003eolder ransomware threat named Warlock Dark Army\u003c/a\u003e.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://research.checkpoint.com/2025/before-toolshell-exploring-storm-2603s-previous-ransomware-operations/\"\u003eResearch published by CheckPoint on July 31\u003c/a\u003e provided further details on Storm-2603’s activities, noting that the group used multiple ransomware payloads and sometimes bundled them together. Payloads were often deployed using DLL sideloading, a popular tactic among Chinese groups. Another feature of its attacks was the use of a custom command and control (C\u0026amp;C) framework that appeared to be called ak47c2 by the attackers themselves.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOther vendors began sharing what they knew about the group. \u003ca href=\"https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/ak47-activity-linked-to-sharepoint-vulnerabilities/\"\u003ePalo Alto Unit 42 said\u003c/a\u003e what it called the Project AK47 toolkit used by Storm-2603 (which it calls CL-CRI-1040) included a backdoor, loaders that were deployed via DLL sideloading, and a ransomware payload called AK47/Anylock. The group also used the legitimate application 7zip (7z.exe) to sideload a loader named 7z.dll. The group had been recently linked to a ransomware site named Warlock Client. It too noted that the group had also been acting as a LockBit 3.0 affiliate.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSimilar activity was uncovered by Symantec and Carbon Black in early August when an engineering company in the Middle East was attacked with Warlock. The attackers also used 7z.exe to sideload a loader named 7z.dll.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://www.trendmicro.com/en_us/research/25/h/warlock-ransomware.html\"\u003eMost recently, Trend Micro published its own findings on Warlock\u003c/a\u003e, which suggested that the Warlock payload may simply be a rebrand of Anylock since it had observed Warlock appending encrypted files with the extension .x2anylock. Trend also threw more light on the LockBit connection, saying that the version of Warlock it analyzed appeared to be a modified version of the LockBit 3.0 payload.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTrend also noted that there may be a link to the now retired Black Basta ransomware operation, observing similarities in tactics, negotiation styles and victimology, suggesting “a possible offshoot or rebrand.”\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTrend’s conclusion, that Warlock may be a rebrand of Anylock, is supported by findings from Symantec and Carbon Black. In an investigation into an attack against a U.S. firm in early August 2025, we found a ransomware payload attempting to encrypt files and appending the extensions .x2anylock, but the ransom note claimed the attack had been performed by Warlock. Again, the attackers sideloaded a malicious file named 7z.dll.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eLinks to earlier espionage attacks\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile Warlock appears to be a rebrand of the older Anylock payload, some of the tools used in Warlock attacks suggest that the group behind Warlock has been active for a lot longer than previously known. In both Warlock attacks investigated by Symantec and Carbon Black in August 2025, the attackers deployed a custom defense evasion tool, which was signed with a stolen digital certificate that appeared to come from a company or developer called coolschool (Serial: 4deb2644a5ad1488f98f6a8d6bca1fab).\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis leveraged a vulnerable driver (SHA256: f6ee01303cf1d68015eee49f7dc7f26151a04ae642a47e49c70806931ce652d3) to try and disable security software on infected systems using the Bring Your Own Vulnerable Driver (BYOVD) technique. The driver was an old Baidu anti-virus driver dating from 2016 with an expired certificate. It was renamed googleapiutil64.sys, likely as a bid to make it appear less suspicious.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis coolschool stolen certificate appears to have been in use as far back as 2022, when it was used to sign Cobalt Strike and BYOVD-related malware uploaded to VirusTotal. In 2022, \u003ca href=\"https://stillu.cc/assets/slides/2023-08-Unmasking%20CamoFei.pdf\"\u003eresearchers at TeamT5 linked the stolen certificate to an APT group they dubbed CamoFe\u003c/a\u003ei, who appeared to be Chinese threat actors that had been active since at least 2019. The group was involved in a diverse range of attacks, ranging from espionage, to denial-of-service, to ransomware. Its ransomware payload, known as CatB, had been signed with the same coolschool certificate.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSimilar attacks continued until at least 2024.\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.sentinelone.com/labs/chamelgang-attacking-critical-infrastructure-with-ransomware/\"\u003eSentinelOne, which calls the group ChamelGang\u003c/a\u003e, said it had staged attacks against organizations in the U.S., Brazil, India, Russia, Taiwan and Japan.\u0026nbsp;This included attacks on the Presidency of Brazil and the All-India Institute of Medical Sciences (AIIMS). Noting the group’s targeting, SentinelOne commented on the blurring of the lines between espionage and cybercrime attacks, noting that in some cases, ransomware attacks could be used to misattribute or cover up evidence of espionage intrusions.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eEmergent China cybercrime nexus\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eAlthough the toolset used by this group has evolved over time, the links to earlier attacks suggest that some, if not all the actors, behind Warlock may have been active since 2019. The diverse range of attacks the group has been involved in suggests they may be contractors, willing to sell their services to entities involved in espionage but also not above generating additional income from ransomware attacks. Indeed, it’s involvement in ransomware may at times be useful to obfuscate or cover up espionage activities.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe involvement of Chinese espionage actors in ransomware is a growing phenomenon. In February of this year, Symantec and Carbon Black uncovered evidence of a Chinese espionage actor, \u003ca href=\"https://www.security.com/threat-intelligence/chinese-espionage-ransomware\"\u003eseemingly moonlighting as an affiliate for RA World ransomware\u003c/a\u003e. The attackers behind Warlock appear to be a different breed, with cybercrime not a sideline, but one of the group’s core activities.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eProtection/Mitigation\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor the latest protection updates, please visit the \u003ca href=\"https://www.broadcom.com/support/security-center/protection-bulletin\"\u003eSymantec Protection Bulletin\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eIndicators of Compromise\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf an IOC is malicious and the file available to us, Symantec Endpoint products will detect and block that file.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e9d52af33c05ea80f9bc47404b02ace4e16203dd81aef9021924885a6bff1d3c1 - Loader (7z.dll)\u003cbr\u003e15649e4d246fe6d03dc75ecb4cabe5d1f8723519ed8dd3176e1a97325e827daf - Loader (7z.dll) 24480dbe306597da1ba393b6e30d542673066f98826cc07ac4b9033137f37dbf - Curl Backdoor\u003cbr\u003ef6ee01303cf1d68015eee49f7dc7f26151a04ae642a47e49c70806931ce652d3 - Warlock\u003c/p\u003e"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"25:T2e87,"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"\u003cp\u003eChina-based attackers used the ToolShell vulnerability (\u003ca href=\"https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/vulnerability/CVE-2025-53770\"\u003eCVE-2025-53770\u003c/a\u003e) to compromise a telecoms company in the Middle East shortly after the vulnerability was publicly revealed and patched in July 2025.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe same threat actors also compromised two government departments in the same African country during the same time period. Zingdoor, which was deployed on the networks of all three organizations, has in the past been associated with the Chinese group Glowworm (aka Earth Estries, FamousSparrow).\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAnother tool used in this campaign, \u003ca href=\"https://www.synacktiv.com/publications/krustyloader-rust-malware-linked-to-ivanti-connectsecure-compromises\"\u003eKrustyLoader\u003c/a\u003e, has also previously been linked to activity by a group called UNC5221,\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/china-nexus-exploiting-critical-ivanti-vulnerability\"\u003ewhich has been described as a China-nexus group\u003c/a\u003e.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe attackers also gained access to the networks of two government agencies in South America and a university in the U.S. recently. In these attacks, the attackers used other vulnerabilities for initial access and exploited SQL servers and Apache HTTP servers running the Adobe ColdFusion software to deliver their malware. Notably, in the South American victims, the attackers used the filename “mantec.exe”, possibly to mimic a Symantec filename (“symantec.exe”) in an attempt to hide their malicious activity. This binary (mantec.exe), which is a legitimate copy of a BugSplat executable, a tool used for bug tracking, was used to sideload a malicious DLL.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eEvidence suggests that a state technology agency in an African country, a government department in the Middle East and a finance company in a European country were also compromised by the same attackers.\u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWhat is ToolShell?\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eToolShell\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.security.com/threat-intelligence/toolshell-zero-day-sharepoint-cve-2025-53770\"\u003ewas patched by Microsoft in July 2025\u003c/a\u003e, but by the time it was patched it had already been exploited in the wild as a zero-day vulnerability. ToolShell affects on-premise SharePoint servers and gives an attacker unauthenticated access to vulnerable servers, allowing them to remotely execute code and access all content and file systems. ToolShell was a variant of another vulnerability (\u003ca href=\"https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/vulnerability/CVE-2025-49704\"\u003eCVE-2025-49704\u003c/a\u003e) that had been patched in July 2025.\u0026nbsp; Another related vulnerability (\u003ca href=\"https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/vulnerability/CVE-2025-53771\"\u003eCVE-2025-53771\u003c/a\u003e) was also patched at the same time as ToolShell. This is a path traversal bug that allows an authorized attacker to perform spoofing over a network. It too was a variant of an older patched vulnerability (\u003ca href=\"https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/vulnerability/CVE-2025-49706\"\u003eCVE-2025-49706\u003c/a\u003e).\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eShortly after patching the vulnerabilities, Microsoft said that\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2025/07/22/disrupting-active-exploitation-of-on-premises-sharepoint-vulnerabilities/\"\u003eat least three Chinese groups had been exploiting ToolShell.\u003c/a\u003e Microsoft said at the time that two Chinese espionage groups had been exploiting the vulnerability - Budworm (aka Linen Typhoon) and Sheathminer (aka Violet Typhoon). In addition to this, a third China-based actor, known as Storm-2603, was also exploiting the vulnerabilities to carry out attacks in which it was\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.darkreading.com/endpoint-security/ransomware-actors-toolshell-sharepoint-bugs\"\u003edistributing the Warlock ransomware\u003c/a\u003e.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eToolset\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eMalicious activity in the telecoms company in the Middle East began on July 21, 2025, just two days after patches were published for ToolShell, with the deployment of a likely webshell by the attackers.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe attackers loaded the Zingdoor backdoor onto the network by sideloading it using a legitimate Trend Micro binary. Zingdoor is a HTTP backdoor written in Go, which was first seen in April 2023, and\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.trendmicro.com/en_us/research/23/h/earth-estries-targets-government-tech-for-cyberespionage.html\"\u003efirst documented by Trend Micro in August that year\u003c/a\u003e being used in a campaign that they attributed to Glowworm. Zingdoor can collect system information, upload and download files, and run arbitrary commands on compromised networks. As well as Zingdoor, the attackers also deployed what appears to be the ShadowPad Trojan. The loader for the Trojan was sideloaded using a legitimate BitDefender binary (SHA256: 3fc4f3ffce6188d3ef676f9825cdfa297903f6ca7f76603f12179b2e4be90134).\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eShadowPad is a modular remote access Trojan (RAT) that is closely associated with China-based APT groups. Because of its modular nature, ShadowPad can be continuously updated with new functionalities. This capability makes it a powerful tool. It is associated with various threat groups, particularly the APT41-nexus groups such as Blackfly, Grayfly and Redfly. It was\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.welivesecurity.com/en/eset-research/you-will-always-remember-this-as-the-day-you-finally-caught-famoussparrow/\"\u003edocumented being used by Glowworm in 2024\u003c/a\u003e, which was the first time that particular group had been observed using the malware. It has more recently been\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.trendmicro.com/en_us/research/25/b/updated-shadowpad-malware-leads-to-ransomware-deployment.html\"\u003eused in attacks where ransomware has been deployed\u003c/a\u003e. Typically, ShadowPad is loaded onto victim networks via DLL sideloading.\u0026nbsp;DLL sideloading is a technique where the attackers use the DLL search order mechanism in Windows to plant and then invoke a legitimate application that executes a malicious DLL payload.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOn July 25, KrustyLoader was dropped by the attackers. KrustyLoader was\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.synacktiv.com/en/publications/krustyloader-rust-malware-linked-to-ivanti-connectsecure-compromises\"\u003efirst documented in January 2024\u003c/a\u003e. It is an initial-stage malware, written in Rust, which has the primary purpose of delivering a second-stage payload. KrustyLoader can carry out various anti-sandbox and anti-analysis checks, can make a copy of itself and set itself up to self-delete when its activity is finished, and can decrypt and download additional malware. Its previous activity has been linked to China-based threat actors, and in earlier campaigns it was also used to download the Sliver post-exploitation framework, which is also seen deployed against this target.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSliver is an\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://github.com/BishopFox/sliver\"\u003eopen-source cross-platform adversary emulation/red team framework\u003c/a\u003e that can legitimately be used for security testing. However, it is often abused by threat actors who use it as a command-and-control framework.\u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eA variety of publicly available and living-off-the-land tools are also used by the attackers in this activity, including:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCertutil\u003c/strong\u003e: \u003ca href=\"https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/previous-versions/windows/it-pro/windows-server-2012-r2-and-2012/cc732443(v=ws.11)\"\u003eMicrosoft Windows utility\u0026nbsp;\u003c/a\u003ethat can be used for various malicious purposes, such as to decode information, to download files, and to install browser root certificates.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eGoGo Scanner\u003c/strong\u003e: A\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://github.com/chainreactors/gogo\"\u003epublicly available\u003c/a\u003e automated scanning engine aimed at Chinese speaking users, for use by red teams. It is available on GitHub.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eRevsocks\u003c/strong\u003e: A publicly available cross-platform\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://github.com/emilarner/revsocks\"\u003eSOCKS5 proxy server program/library\u003c/a\u003e written in C that can also reverse itself over a firewall.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eProcdump\u003c/strong\u003e: \u003ca href=\"https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/sysinternals/downloads/procdump\"\u003eMicrosoft Sysinternals tool\u003c/a\u003e for monitoring an application for CPU spikes and generating crash dumps, but can also be used as a general process dump utility.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMinidump\u003c/strong\u003e: A script from the post-exploitation framework PowerSploit used for dumping processes. Attackers usually dump lsass.exe to find credentials.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eLsassDumper\u003c/strong\u003e: A\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://github.com/mendax0110/lsassDumper\"\u003eutility\u003c/a\u003e designed to dump the Local Security Authority Subsystem Service (LSASS) process memory to a file.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eAn exploit for the Windows LSA Spoofing Vulnerability,\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://msrc.microsoft.com/update-guide/en-US/vulnerability/CVE-2021-36942\"\u003eCVE-2021-36942\u003c/a\u003e (aka PetitPotam), was also executed. PetitPotam is an exploitation technique that allows for a threat actor within a compromised network to steal credentials and authentication information from Windows Servers such as a Domain Controller to gain full control of the domain. This is likely used for lateral movement or privilege escalation.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eToolShell impact further revealed\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThese attacks demonstrate that the ToolShell vulnerability was being exploited by an even wider range of Chinese threat actors than was originally thought.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThere is some overlap in the types of victims and some of the tools used between this activity and activity previously attributed to Glowworm. However, we do not have sufficient evidence to conclusively attribute this activity to one specific group, though we can say that all evidence points to those behind it being China-based threat actors.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe large number of apparent victims of this activity is also notable. This may indicate that the attackers were carrying out an element of mass scanning for the ToolShell vulnerability, before then carrying out further activity only on networks of interest. The activity carried out on targeted networks indicates that the attackers were interested in stealing credentials and in establishing persistent and stealthy access to victim networks, likely for the purpose of espionage.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eIndicators of Compromise (IOCs)\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFile indicators\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e6240e39475f04bfe55ab7cba8746bd08901d7678b1c7742334d56f2bc8620a35 - One of its intrusions was on the network of a Russian IT service provider and lasted for the first five months of 2025.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAttackers had access to code repository and software build systems that they could potentially leverage to carry out supply chain attacks targeting the company’s customers in Russia. Notably too, the attackers were exfiltrating data to Yandex Cloud. Yandex is a popular service in Russia, so the attackers likely chose to use it in order to avoid raising suspicions.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn other activity on a large South American government organization in July 2025, Jewelbug deployed a new backdoor that appears to be under development by the group.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug also compromised the network of a Taiwanese company, as well as another IT provider based in South Asia in recent months. However, its targeting of a Russian company is of particular note as Chinese and Russian threat actors have, until recently, rarely been seen to be attacking each other. Jewelbug’s attack is the continuation of a trend that seems to have begun following Russia’s invasion of Ukraine.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAttack on Russian IT service provider\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe first suspicious activity that occurred on this network was the appearance of a file named 7zup.exe (Command line: \u003cem\u003eCSIDL_PROFILE\\public\\downloads\\7zup.exe -d -remote up\u003c/em\u003e), which is a renamed copy of cdb.exe, a benign Microsoft signed binary. CDB is the Microsoft Console Debugger. Use of a renamed version of cbd.exe is a hallmark of Jewelbug activity. CDB can be used to run shellcode and bypass application whitelisting. It can also be used to launch executables, run DLLs and terminate security solutions, making it a powerful tool. Microsoft recommends that CDB should be blocked from running by default and whitelisted for specific users only when it’s explicitly needed.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOther activity on this network included credential dumping, and persistence and elevation of privileges via scheduled tasks (schtasks). The attackers also attempted to cover up their activity by clearing Windows Event Logs.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe use of Yandex Cloud to exfiltrate data was also a probable attempt by the attackers to remain under the radar as Yandex is a legitimate and commonly used cloud service in Russia. For this reason, it is unlikely to be blocked by Russian enterprises, and its use is less likely to raise suspicions. To exfiltrate the victims’ data the attackers used a malicious sample that they had named “yandex2.exe.”\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAs mentioned previously, the attackers were also targeting machines with build systems and code repository systems, potentially seeking to leverage access to the source code to carry out a supply chain attack targeting the company’s customers in Russia. IT service providers are popular targets for attackers seeking to carry out supply chain attacks as they often have extensive access to their customers’ systems and may be able to automatically deploy updates or software across a large number of networks simultaneously, potentially giving the attackers access to, or allowing them to infect, a huge number of organizations at the same time.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt appears attackers may have been on this network for some time, with the first indication of suspicious activity dating from January 2025, while the most recent suspicious activity on this network occurred in May 2025.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eNew backdoor deployed in South American victim\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug also compromised a network belonging to a South American government department. It appears Jewelbug has been on the network of the department multiple times, or else has maintained persistence on the network for a long time, because suspicious activity was first seen in this organization in September 2024, with the most recent activity seen in July 2025.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn the September 2024 activity, the attackers attempted to add a new user to the network, as well as attempting to deploy a remote access tool to gain access to machines. They also used the legitimate AnyDesk remote management software, and deployed the 7-zip archive manager, which is often used to pack files before they are exfiltrated from victim machines. In the more recent activity in July 2025, the attackers used a legitimate executable for DLL sideloading, and the SMBExec tool for likely lateral movement on the network. They also used scheduled tasks for persistence, and BITSAdmin and the curl tool, probably to exfiltrate data.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAlso in this organization, Jewelbug deployed what appears to be a new backdoor in development by the group.\u0026nbsp;This malware leverages Microsoft Graph API and OneDrive as its command and control (C\u0026amp;C) servers. The malware deployed on the victim network appears to have some issues and limitations, perhaps pointing to it being a work in progress, or some inexperience on the part of the developer. However, activity we do see being carried out by this malware includes:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eObtains a list of files from targeted machines and uploads this to OneDrive\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eDoes some logging into C:\\ProgramData\\application.ini. Some examples of this logging includes:\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003einit successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eFreeFileInformation Fuc successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eC:\\Users\\Public\\Libraries~\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCreateDirectory\u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp;Successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eGet Token successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCreate Folder In OneDrive successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eHttpSendRequestWPtr Error Code:0\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCreates the directory: C:\\Users\\Public\\Libraries~ and hides it on the victim machine\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe malware also obtains the infected machine’s IP, Windows version, and hostname. In some cases, it also collects the machine identifier. It uploads this information to OneDrive.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe logging done by this malware is of note as it points to this malware possibly being “tested” by the attackers. It is also notable as it shows that Jewelbug is continuing to develop new malware. The\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.security.com/threat-intelligence/cloud-espionage-attacks\"\u003euse of Microsoft Graph API\u003c/a\u003e and OneDrive for C\u0026amp;C by Jewelbug is also interesting as it minimizes malicious indicators that would be observable to traditional security software, making this activity much harder to detect.\u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eBYOVD technique used to target Taiwanese software company\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug was also on the network of a Taiwanese software company in October and November 2024. Unlike the later activity targeting the Russian IT service provider, however, there was no evidence of a software supply chain attack motivation. The attackers used DLL sideloading to load malware payloads. DLL sideloading is a very popular tactic among Chinese threat groups. ShadowPad was also deployed on this network. ShadowPad is a powerful modular backdoor that is exclusively used by Chinese threat actors. The attackers also used the KillAV tool to disable security software, as well as deploying a publicly available tool called EchoDrv, which permits abuse of the Kernel read/write vulnerability in the ECHOAC anti-cheat driver. This is likely an example of the attackers using the bring-your-own-vulnerable-driver (BYOVD) technique as an attempt to avoid their malicious activity being detected. They also created scheduled tasks for persistence.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe attackers leveraged LSASS and Mimikatz for dumping credentials, and Fast Reverse Proxy, which can expose local servers to the public internet, while they also deployed several publicly available tools for discovery and privilege escalation. The tools (PrintNotifyPotato, Coerced Potato, and Sweet Potato) are all freely available on GitHub. The attackers also used a publicly available tunnelling tool called Earthworm in a likely attempt to mask commands or data being sent to and from the victim network. The attackers were on this network for approximately three weeks, indicating some skill at remaining under the radar.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOn the networks of both the Taiwanese software company and the South American government agency, a renamed version of the Microsoft Console Debugger (cdb.exe) tool was used. Jewelbug’s use of this tool is notable as it is a relatively under the radar tool, despite it having numerous uses that could be beneficial to malicious actors, as discussed above. In one instance we also saw the CDB tool being injected into mspaint.exe. Mspaint has \u003ca href=\"https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/advanced-backdoor-squidoor/\"\u003epreviously been documented\u003c/a\u003e as being used by Jewelbug to inject malware.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug’s preference for using cloud services and other legitimate tools in its operations indicates that remaining under the radar and establishing a stealthy and persistent presence on victim networks is of utmost importance to this group.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003ePrevious Jewelbug activity\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug is believed to have been active since mid-2023, which was when binaries associated with it were first uploaded to VirusTotal. As well as the attacks mentioned in this blog, Jewelbug has also \u003ca href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security-labs/fragile-web-ref7707\"\u003ebeen associated\u003c/a\u003e with attacks targeting organizations in Southeast Asia, including a university, telecoms organization and a government ministry. Up to this point the group has been associated only with attacks on organizations in Asia and South America, there has been no previous documentation of the group targeting organizations in Russia. A \u003ca href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security-labs/fragile-web-ref7707\"\u003eblog released by security company Elastic earlier this year\u003c/a\u003e did say it had seen Jewelbug attackers using a domain that mimicked a Russian organization, but they did not identify any victims in Russia as part of that activity.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat blog also detailed Jewelbug’s use of unique malware called Finaldraft, Pathloader and Guidloader. Finaldraft is a full-featured remote administration tool with the ability to accept add-on modules that extend functionality. It also:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eHas support for proxying network traffic.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eUses third-party Microsoft Graph API for C\u0026amp;C.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eSupports both Windows and Linux versions.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003ePathloader and Guidloader are malware used to download and execute encrypted shellcode in memory. They have only been observed in association with Finaldraft.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/advanced-backdoor-squidoor/\"\u003ePalo Alto also published a report\u003c/a\u003e about the custom malware used by Jewelbug, though it dubbed the Finaldraft backdoor Squidoor. It also noted that Jewelbug gained access to networks of interest by exploiting various vulnerabilities in Internet Information Services (IIS) servers before deploying webshells on infected servers. It also said that as well as using the Microsoft Graph API for C\u0026amp;C communication, the attackers also used DNS and ICMP tunnelling.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003ePrevious reporting about Jewelbug has also noted the use of the CDB tool, the use of mspaint.exe to inject commands, and the wide-ranging use of dual-use and living off the land tools by the attackers as well, all of which was also seen in the recent activity observed by the Threat Hunter Team.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAll indications point to Jewelbug being of Chinese origin, with its motivation most likely to be espionage and maintaining a long-term and stealthy presence on compromised networks.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eChinese threat actors looking in a new direction?\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe most notable element of all this recent Jewelbug activity is the targeting of a Russian IT service provider by the Chinese APT group. When it comes to things like the Russia-Ukraine conflict and other geopolitical matters, China has traditionally backed, or at least not opposed, Russia, with the two considered to be loosely allied. The targeting of a Russian organization by a Chinese APT group shows, however, that Russia is not out-of-bounds when it comes to operations by China-based actors. The fact that there are indications the IT service provider may have been targeted for the purposes of a software supply chain attack on the company’s customers in Russia is also notable as it means this attack had the potential to give the attackers access to a large number of companies in the country, which they could have used for cyber espionage or disruption.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug’s use of a new backdoor-in-development in this set of activity is also important as it shows that the group is continuing to actively develop its toolset and capabilities. Jewelbug, as a relatively new Chinese APT group, is one to watch as it has the skills to develop its own malware and maintain a long-term and stealthy presence on networks.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eIndicators of compromise (IOCs)\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFile 2\u0026gt;\u0026amp;1\u003c/p\u003e"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"27:T1dde,"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"\u003cp\u003eAttackers deploying the LockBit ransomware have continually evolved their tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs) to evade detection and maximize its impact. Among their sophisticated arsenal, two techniques stand out for their effectiveness in concealing malicious activities: DLL sideloading and masquerading. This blog post delves into how attackers deploying LockBit leverage these methods to establish persistence and blend into legitimate system processes.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile this blog details methods used in LockBit ransomware attacks specifically, similar methods are used by attackers deploying other malicious payloads as well.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eDLL sideloading: Exploiting trust for malicious gain\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eDLL sideloading is a stealthy attack technique where a legitimate application is tricked into loading a malicious Dynamic Link Library (DLL) instead of a legitimate one. This often occurs when a legitimate application attempts to load a DLL from an unsecured location or when the malicious DLL has the same name as a legitimate one but is placed in a directory that is searched earlier by the operating system.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eLockBit has been observed using DLL sideloading to execute its payload. Here's a simplified breakdown of the process:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eLegitimate application as a loader:\u003c/strong\u003e LockBit often bundles its malicious DLL with a legitimate, digitally signed application. This application, when executed, will attempt to load specific DLLs required for its functionality.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMalicious DLL placement:\u003c/strong\u003e The LockBit threat actors place their malicious DLL (often with a filename identical to a legitimate one) in a directory that is searched by the legitimate application before the legitimate DLL's actual location.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eExecution of malicious code:\u003c/strong\u003e When the legitimate application is launched, it inadvertently loads and executes the malicious DLL, allowing the ransomware to initiate its encryption process or other nefarious activities under the guise of a trusted program.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis technique is particularly effective because it leverages the trust placed in legitimate applications, making it harder for traditional security solutions to differentiate between legitimate and malicious activity.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eMasquerading: Blending in with legitimate processes\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eMasquerading refers to the technique of disguising malicious files or processes to appear as legitimate system files or applications. LockBit actors employ various masquerading tactics to avoid detection and maintain persistence on compromised systems.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eCommon masquerading techniques observed in LockBit attacks include:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eRenaming malicious files:\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;Threat actors rename their ransomware executables or associated files to common system filenames (e.g., svchost.exe, explorer.exe, cmd.exe). This makes it difficult for users or even some security tools to distinguish them from legitimate system processes.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSpoofing process names:\u003c/strong\u003e LockBit may also attempt to manipulate process names to appear as legitimate services or applications running on the system. This can be achieved by injecting code into legitimate processes or by using specific APIs to alter process information.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eUsing legitimate icons:\u003c/strong\u003e To further enhance their disguise, LockBit samples have been found to use icons identical to those of legitimate Windows applications, making the malicious files appear less suspicious to a casual observer.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eLeveraging legitimate directories:\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;Malicious files are often placed in directories commonly used by legitimate system files, such as C:\\Windows\\System32 or C:\\ProgramData, further aiding in their camouflage.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn recent LockBit cases, attackers have been observed leveraging trusted applications alongside malicious DLLs (disguised with legitimate names) placed in the same directory. The technique relies on DLL side-loading, where the legitimate application inadvertently loads the malicious DLL. Examples include:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eJarsigner.exe and jli.dll\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003eBoth are parts of the Java platform. Jarsigner.exe naturally loads jli.dll. In this case, the attacker placed a legitimate jarsigner.exe and a malicious jli.dll (disguised with the correct name) in the same folder. When jarsigner.exe was executed, it loaded the malicious jli.dll, which acted as a loader for further payload execution.\u003cbr\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMpCmdRun.exe and mpclient.dll\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003eThese components belong to Windows Defender. The attacker dropped a renamed MpCmdRun.exe (masquerading as \u0026lt;company_domain_name\u0026gt;.exe) along with a malicious mpclient.dll. When \u0026lt;company_domain_name\u0026gt;.exe was run, it loaded mpclient.dll, which contained the LockBit ransomware payload and began encrypting files.\u003cbr\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eClink_x86.exe and clink_dll_x86.dll\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003eThese are part of the Clink tool, which integrates with cmd.exe to enhance command-line input using its own Readline-based features. The attacker dropped a renamed Clink_x86.exe (again masquerading as \u0026lt;company_domain_name\u0026gt;.exe) alongside a malicious clink_dll_x86.dll. Upon execution, \u0026lt;company_domain_name\u0026gt;.exe loaded the malicious DLL clink_dll_x86.dll, triggering the LockBit ransomware payload and starting file encryption.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn the following section, we’ll take a closer look into how the LockBit attacker is leveraging these techniques at various stages of the attack chain in recently seen attacks.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAttack chain:\u0026nbsp;Initial access\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eA recent LockBit attack started with the attackers accessing machines using well-known remote desktop tools like MeshAgent, TeamViewer etc. Once an attacker gets access to remote machines, files are uploaded directly and executed.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAttack chain: Privilege escalation\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eNSSM:\u003c/strong\u003e \u003ca href=\"https://nssm.cc/\"\u003eNSSM\u003c/a\u003e\u0026nbsp;was used to run a remote access Trojan (RAT) as a service. We also saw this RAT renamed as o.exe.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePsExec:\u003c/strong\u003e PsExec was used to run cmd as a SYSTEM user using the “-s” parameter\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ccode\u003ePsExec64.exe -s -i cmd\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAttack chain:\u0026nbsp;Discovery\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eNet.exe, nltest.exe and query.exe were used to get details about domain users, domains group, and group permissions.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003enet user \u0026lt;username\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003enet group \"domain computers\" /do\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003enltest /domain_trusts\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003equery user\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAttack chain: Credential theft\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eTokenUtils.exe was used to steal tokens from all users and use those user tokens to launch any applications.
